Special DiFpatch to The Call.
SANTA CRUZ. June 13.-At a point
6.bcut one hundred yards this side of the
entrance to tunnel No. 6 on the narrow
gauge line to San Francisco and one mile
from Kelton a serious train wreck oc
curred a few minutes after 2 o'clock this
afternoon. Engineer James Stanley and
Fireman Henry Coyle were severely in
jured, but the fifty passengers in the cars
were not hurt.
The train consisted of seven coaches and
left the station about ten minutes late. It
was making up the lost time on the run
to Felton. After swinging around the
trestle curve before reaching the tunnel
the locomotive jumped the track and
bumped over the ties for nearly 100 feet.
Then It struck against the wall of granite
en the left side of the road and stopped
with a crash that shivered every part of
it? mechanism. The smokestack was car
ried away, projecting bowlders tore off
the roof of the cab and the tender, which
had become detached from the engine,
toppled into the gorge of the San Lorenzo
River, a hundred feet below. The for
ward trucks of the baggage car left the
rails end the front end and side of the
car were broken by contact with the
rocks. The passenger coaches remained
en the track.
Rescue of Injured Men.
The terrified passengers rushed to the
platforms of the cars in a state of panic,
and several had to be restrained from
leaping into the gorge. The train men
hurried to the engine and found Fireman
Coyle pinned down by the wheels of the
fcasrgafre car. It took twenty minutes to
extricate Mm. KIs leps were crushed, his
left eye was partially gouged out and he
was suffering from internal injuries. En
pir.eer Stanley, who was lying alongside
the engine, was also injured internally
nrd was bleeding from a deep scalp
v.oun<3. Dr. Mear^s of Philadelphia was
on the train, ana with the meager mate
rial at hand did all he could to alleviate
the suffering- of the men. Miss Kate
White of Oakland, who had been visiting
her aunt. Mrs. F\ W. Swanton, in Santa
Cruz and was returning home, did excel
lent service for the injured. She had her
trunk opened and tore up some of her
wearing apparel to make bandages.
Word was immediately sent to the pow
der works, two rr.i'.ep away, and for a few
hours about forty of the men were spared
from the works to clear the wreck. They
brought medical supplies and did much to
aid the injured trainmen. Dr. Morgan
and Dr. Knight of Santa Cruz were early
at the scene. While assisting: in the res
cue work H. Foster, a merchant of this
city, his hack.
Mystery About the Cause.
The cause of the ace'dent is not known.
Trackwalker Muthern declares that no
rocks had fallen on the railF. It is con
cert ured by some that the tender was so
heavily laden that in swinging around the
curve it swayed more than usual and iost
Its balance.
J. F. Maloney. United States mall clerk
on the wrecked train, considers himself
the luckiest man living. In an Interview
he stated:
"I noticed that we were going at an
tnusral speed for that part of the' road
and leaned out the Fide door to ascertain
the cause. Juct as I looked out we struck
the sharp curve at the south entrance of
tunnel No. <5. and the tender left the
track. I went tr. the opposite door with
the intention of jumping, and as I did so
the mail car shot past the tender. Had
I jumped I would have been instantly
crushed betwÂ«<?n the mail car and the
tender."
The injured engineer and fireman were
conveyed to the Southern Pacific Hos
pital In San Francisco. At Glenwood they
were Joined by Fireman Coyle's father,
who accompanied them on the remainder
of their journey.

Special Dispatch to The Call.
MANILA. June 13.â Prince Poniatowskl
of San Francisco has procured from the
Sultan of Jolo an absolute concession for
fifty years, ending with the year 1950, of
the island of Paragua. It Is reported the
Prince has formed a $10,000,000 company
and is making plans for a most thorough
exploitation of the timber, mining, rubber
and pearl industries of the island and the
development of its shipping. The' scheme
also embodies a plan for the building of
roads and harbor Improvements.
The representative of Prince Poniatow
&ki here, John Anthony, presented docu
ments to the Philippine Commission to
day and asked for an indorsement of the
plan. The question involves the agroe
ment "of General Bates w,ith the Sultan
and also the validity of the Sultan's juris
diction and authority, which occupied an
uncertain status with Spain and after
ward with the United States. The com-
Washington Officials Will Pass Upon
the Validity of the Extensive
Concession.

1884 he served with the "Black Watch," taking part in '.the

tiations in Berlin on the bounary question of: the Oil Rivers

Decrepit Squaw Acts

County.
Sl>eclal Dispatch to The Call.
SAN BERNARDINO, June 13.-Roy
Baxter, aged 17 years, and May Green
lee f. who has seen nineteen sweet sum
mer?, have been lovers for several
months. When the subject of their mar
riage was broached to their parents the
latter strenuously objected. The young
people appeared willing to abide by the
decision of their elders, and little more
was thought of the love match outside the
charmed circle of the lovers. It now ap
pears to have been another case of love
rinding the way without troubling the
locksmith.
After taking a walk last night -the two
young people decided to outwit the stern
parents and this morning boarded an
<-arly train for Los Angeles, where they
Expected to procure a license and be mar
ried. The father of the prospective groom
learned of their plans late this evening
and immediately started to Los Angeles
for the purpose of preventing the union if
possible. The young man, being- under age.
may not be given the license unless it Is
obtained under false pretenses.
Yesterday Clarence Baxter, a brother of
Roy Baxter. In company with Belle An
derson, a petite brunette of 17 years,
eloped to Riverside, where the Rev. A. E.
Hawgood united them in marriage.
REDDING, June 13.â Although afflicted
with- blindness for years and old and de
crepit. Kate Fernondo, an Indian woman,
the common law wife of an invalid Span
iard living seven miles west of Redding,
had enough presence of mind last night to
tear her clothing off and jump into a near
by creek, thus saving herself from an aw
ful death by flre. : Â»
Despite her affliction the woman for the
past few years .has been better able to
work than her sick husband, and has
made a living for the two by gardening.
Last evening she attempted to burn dry
grass away from the vicinity of their
home. The fire got away from her, and in
her efforts to place it under, control she
set fire to her dress.
With a wild scream the blind woman
tore off all her clothing, and running to a
creek near by jumped into the water Ind
suffered only a few slight burns. A por
tion of the house was destroyed, but the
husband was not Injured. Fortunately
for him, the blind woman succeeded in
extinguishing the flames after she had
saved herself in the creek. The fire
burned for three -miles north and a mile
south, endangering numerous miners'
cabins, which were only saved by hard
effort. This morning the blaze was work-
Ing rapidly east toward town.

LOS ANGELES, June 13.â Thirteen
statesmen who help to guide the destinies
of the nation. spent a few hours at San
to-day on their tour of Inspection
of the big breakwater and the inner and
outer harbors. Four members of the Riv
ers and Harbors Committee were absent
when a special train over the Southern
Pacific Railroad pulled out of the Arcade
station for San Pedro at 9:30 o'clock.
The inspection, party was composed of
Theodore E. Burton of Cleveland, Ohio
chairman: Walter Reeves of Streator, 111
Roswell P. Bi.shop of Ludington, Mich :
Erne F. Acheson of Washington Pa Â¦
Page Morris of Duluth, Minn.; De Alva.
S. Alexander of Buffalo, N. Y.; Thomas
H. Tongue of Hillsboro, Or.; George P
Lawrence of North Adams, Mass. ; James
H. Davidson of Oshkosh, Wis.; Rufus E
Lester of Savannah, Ga.; James H. Bank
head of Fayette.s Ala.; Philip D. McCul
loch of Marlanr.a, Ark. ; Albert S. Berry
of Newport, Ky.; Frank- H. Hitchcock,
chief of the bureau of foreign markets of
the Department of Agriculture; J W
Hencke, secretary to Chairman Burton'
and Lyman M. Ellis, secretary to the
Rivers and Harbors Committee. A dele
gation of business men and members of
the Chamber of Commerce, accompanied
the visiting Congressmen.
Arriving at San Pedro the members of
the party were. taken by rail out to the
point where work is under way on the
breakwater. The big steam derrick and
a score of workmen were unloading rock
at the time of the arrival , of the party
and continued operations during its stay
in order that the Congressmen might
clearly understand how the work is per
formed. Captain. Meyler explained the
character and extent. of the work yet to
be performed. He said that 28,000 tons of
rock were dumped into Â¦ the breakwater
during May. This month the contractors
expect to dump 35,000 tons into the work.
Captain , Meyler explained that this ' will
meet . the requirements of the contract
with the Government. To all appear
ances the work is progressing as rapidly
and satisfactorily as could be. expected.
Many of the members of-* the Con
gressional committee expressed surprise
at the magnitude of the. undertaking.
Leaving the breakwater, steam launches
were boarded for a trip through the inner
harbor. During the trip about the inner
harbor Chairman Burton dictated to his
secretary frequent impressions of things
that attracted his attention. In -fact,
throughout the entire trip the chairman
kept his stenographer by his side and con
tinued to dictate to him page after page
of notes. The only time that Congress
man Burton actually ceased his- labors
was when he sat down to luncheon in the
Brighton Beach Hotel. .'
Concluding the trip about the inner har
bor the members of the party boarded
the steamer 'Falcon and were taken for a
trip about the outer harbor, passing tho
breakwater and "starting on the return
Journey nearly opposite ' Point Firmin.
This trip gave the members- of the board
a splendid opportunity to view the break
water and to take observations of the ex
tent and character of the â¢ operations.

Breakwater Near Los
Angeles.

CHINESE POUR
OVER THE LINE
A Startling Increase in
Smuggling in the
Northwest.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
VANCOUVER. B. C. June 13.â After a
week of investigation at the boundary
line Chief Inspector Dunn of the Immi
gration Department on the Pacific Coast
has decided to recommend that special
commissioners be sent out from Washing
ton to take steps toward stopping the
enormous a.mount of unlawful smuggling
of Chinese and goods across the boundary
line into the United States.
There are at the present time only four
inspectors to guard as many hundred
miles of territory, and the object of the
special commission to go into the matter
will be to arrange a proper patrol sys
tem and make suggestions to the Govern
ment for the guarding of the Mexican
border as well as the one at British Co
lumbia. Dunn says that Chinese turn up
in California who have been shipped in
bond through Vancouver to Mexico, hav
ing broken through the southern bound
ary.
Inspector White, who has been here for
some time under Commissioner Healey,
acted as Dunn's assistant in making the
investigations. He has just made the ar
rest of two Italians and a third man who
had hired them in Canada to work on
railroads in Washington State. They
were the first of a large number to go
through and the whole gang was effect
ively stopped. The inspector was dis
guised as a rancher and held the party up
at the point of a revolver, Â¦which was the
only chance he had of capturing them.
Commissioner White says that the Chi
nese go across the line all the time, and
most of those who land in Vancouver
eventually make their way through to
California. There are from 100 to 200 land
ed here by every steamer and the number
in Vancouver does not grow large. The
officers also say that with Chinese them
selves and Oriental goods a large smug
gling business is carried on between here
and Puget Sound cities. The officials even
have information that the smugglers have
retained one of the best lawyers in the
city to advise them and keep them posted
on the operations of the authorities as
well as to protect them if they are
pounced upon. There is but one revenue
cutter in the local service and officials
state there is little chance of catching tho
offenders under present conditions..
It Is also the intention of Dunn to-Â«sk
the Washington authorities to endeavor
to solve the problem by handling it with
Dominion Government officials. The ille
gal traffic at the boundary has reached
such a serious state that it is almost
equally to the Interest of both countries
that something should be done.

PETALUMA, ' June 13.â W. M. Mat
thews, a blacksmith, employed in the ma

was caught in a drilling lathe to-day and

his load on the wagon.

FALLING HAY BALE KILLS;
A FOUNTAIN QBOVE MAN
Ray Clark, One of the Proprietors of
the Sonoma County Institu
tion, Dead.
SANTA ROSA, Juno 13.-Ray B. Clark,
one of the proprietors of the famous
Fountain Grove Vineyard Company,"
whose property is located near Santa
Rosa, was accidentally killed this after
noon while storing hay In one of the vine
yard company's barns. Clark was direct-
Ing the work of placing the hay in one of
the upper stories when one of the bales,
weighing several hundred pounds, was re
leased before it could be securely placed
and rolled down. It struck Clark on the
head. He was rendered unconscious and
died shortly afterward from the effects
of his Injuries. Clark was about CO years
old and a native of New York. He was
widely known and was wealthy.
and ranks high in wealth among New
York capitalists. His insane wife was
Ida Shrouds, daughter of a Philadelphia
clergyman and an actress. Flagler mar
ried her in 1883, before he had amassed
millions. She was poor. In 1899 she was
declared a victim of chronic insanity and
committed to the sanitarium.
It is said Flagler spend- $1000 a month to
keep his wife in the luxury In which she
lives at the sanitarium. The story goes
that every night the demented woman
adorns herself, expecting a visit from her
husband. He used to visit her frequent
ly at the sanitarium, but she exhibited no
sign of recognition of him.
This Is the second time recently that

Mr. Foote has brought suit, for divorce
from his wife. He name* us co-respond
ents Henry' M. Flakier. John H. Maiden
and J. O'Bannon. Mrs. Foote married

DEVOTE LIVES
TO INCURABLES
Seven Catholic Women
Earn the Cross of
Calvary.
? â
Consecrate Themselves to
Services in Aid of Can
cer Victims.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
NEW YORK, June 13.â Archbishop Cor
rlgan has bestowed tho decoration of the
Cross of Calvary upon seven women
prominent in the good works of the "Ro
man Catholic Church in' New York. The
order of the Dames de Calvare has here- I
tofore not been represented In. this coun- j
try, except by Mrs. Anna Blount Starrs,
who spent flf teen months in the Brus
sels house of the order. The new mem
bers will serve in the house of Calvary,
which Mrs. Starrs founded last year for
the care of poor women afflicted with In
curable cancer and kindred diseases.
The new members are Mrs. Thomas
Francis Meagher, Mrs. S. Goston Uall
ieff, Mrs. George liecker, Mrs. -Callxte
Hnrvier, Mrs. Joseph J. O'Donohue, Mrs.
Thomas Howard Peterson and Mrs. Sarah
A. Carroll. Mrs. Meagher 1b the presi
dent of the house and Mrs. Balltett the
vice-president. Mrs. Starrs Is the
mother superior. The order Is a' secular
one. . -
Their vow was that of consecration to
the work of alleviating human suffering.
They do not renounce the world. Cer
tain members of the order visit the house
daily. They wait upon patients and
wash their wounds. To do this they are
obliged first to take a course of In
struction from nurses of the cancer hos
pital.
Mrs. Peterson, who Is a member of the
prominent Peterson family of Phlladel
phlu, is the only new member of the or-
Oev who has as yet acquired this ability.
Only widows are admitted to the order,
which was founded in France by Mme.
Gamier.
The ceremonies attendant on the con
ferring of the decoration took place at
the Perry-street house on "Wednesday,
when also Archbishop Corrlgan blessed
an addition to the house, which has been
bought through contributions given by
wealthy Catholics to Mrs. Starrs.
Fathers Myhan and O'Flynn assisted
the Archbishop, who wore a white stole
sent by the mother of the house in Lyons,
France. The altar in the chapel will be
covered with linen from the same house.
When the alterations . are made the !
house will accommodate twenty-five pa- ,'
titnts. As they are incurable, they are
not received at regular hospitals, and |
such institutions as this are their only
refuge. Mrs. Catherine Dell, a trained j
cancer nurse from the cancer hospital, Is.
regularly in attendance on the patients.

American Gunners "Win Their Third
LONDON/, June 13.â In spite of high
winds and dull skies the American gun
ners at the international clay bird trap
shooting match, which began at Hendon
Tuesday, continued to-day to show their
superiority over the British. W. R Cros
by killed the first twenty straight and
killed seventeen out of the second twenty
E. H. Tripp got thirty-six out of forty and
J. A. R. Elliott thirty-seven oUt of forty.
At 4 p. m. the Bcore for the day was*
Americans 384. British S48.
The Americans won the third consecu
tive day of the clay bird match by a
score of 843 to 739. The result was ac
cepted by the greatest good will on the
part of their British opponents, the ladies
in the stand waving American and British
flags. Speeches were afterward made by
Captain T. A. Marshall for the United
States and by Captain Butt for the Brit
ish. -

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning- tides are given in the left
hand column Â° and the successive tides of the
day in the order of occurrence as to time of
day; the third time column gives the last tide
of the day. except when there are but three
tides, as sometimes occurs. The heights given
are In addition to the soundings on the United
States Coast Survey charts, except when a
minus (â ) sign precedes the height, and then
the number given Is subtracted from the depth
given by the charts. The plane of reference ia
the mean of the lower low waters.

During the steamship's run from Mazat
lan Juan Buzo was ordered to clean down
one of the masts and refused. When he
grew insolent C.iptain Judkins ordered
; him locked up. Then Daniel .Frias. took
| up the cudgel in behalf oÂ£ his ohipmate
and asked the iest of the crew to mutiny
and release Uieir comrade. He also was
placed in irons, and then there was peace
-and quietness. The' men were turned over
to the harbor police, but when the mat
ter was referred to the British Consul he
said the men would have to be taken back
to Chile, as there was no agreement be
tween the United States and Great Brit
ain by which mutineers on a British ves
sel can be tried in an American court.
Frias and Buzo will therefore be taken
back to Valparaiso for trial. "
The' cabin passengers on the Colombia
were:
Enrique Z. Prieto. Carlos Miron, J. J.
Thomas. Mrs. F. King and child. F. Warren,
E. H. Coffey, E. R. Cuthbert and wife, R. R.
Stevenson, J. V. Tonnell. C. Timmons and J.
B. Dennis.
Besides these there were six steerage
and two Chinese in transit.
Among the steerage passengers were L.
Veliaino and his wife. The man is a phys
ical wreck and any excitement nearly
drives him distracted. Vellaino is a Swiss,
and In company with a number of his
countrymen went to Chile to make a for
tune. They settled in a small place called
Victoria, "about a day's journey from
Talcuhuano. Vellaino had a little money,
and that fact soon reached the ears of the
bandits in the hills.. They swooped down
on the settlement, seized Vellaino's son,
gers Is a Physical Wreck.
The Pacific Steam Navigation Com
pany's Colombia arrived from South and
Central American ports yesterday with
two of her crew in irons. One of the pas
sengers is a physical wreck from having
witnessed the murder of his son by ban
dits in Chile".
STEAMSHIP COLOMBIA HEBE-
One of Her South American Passen-
FOR the first time in its history
San Francisco has three battle
ships and a second class cruiser
in addition to other warlike craft
in the bay. The battleship Iowa
has been at anchor off the transport
wharf for some time. A week ago the
Philadelphia came down from Mare Isl
and. Wednesday afternoon the famous
battleship Oregon came in from China via
Honolulu, and during the night the new
battleship Wisconsin arrived from Puget
Sound. The latter vessel made the splen
did run down the coast of sixty-five hours
from Port Orchard. That is an average
of about thirteen knots an hour for the
run, but there were hours when the bat
tleship logged her eighteen knots. Of
course, everything was in her favor, as
she is in perfect working order and had a
strong northwester behind her all the way
down.
The Iowa- and Wisconsin are the war.
vessels closest in shore. The former is
about off the transport wharf, while the
Wisconsin is oft Harriso"n-street wharf.
Out in midstream and almost between the
Iowa and Wisconsin lies the cruiser Phil
adelphia, while south of her and almost
in a line with Steuart-street wharf lies
the Oregon.
Never again in a time of peace may
such an aggregation of fighting machines
be seen in this port. Even now the Wis
consin is taking on her munitions of war
preparatory to starting for the Orient.
The Philadelphia is ready to sail for Sa
moa and will probably get away on the
ISth inst. The Iowa is to pay a visit to
all Puget Sound ports in a week or so.
and, last of all. the Oregon will go out of
commission. The chances are that she
will be' dismantled and put on the dry-:
dock for permanent repairs. The wounds
made by the rocks in the Gulf of Pechili
were only patched up. and a permanent
job has to be done. The turrets are to be
changed and a new battery Installed, so
when she is again in service she will be a
different Oregon from the one that went
into battle at Santiago. Â¦
Before the month is out the Government
expects to have between 500 and 600 school
teachers- on the way to Manila. At first
it was intended to fit the Sherman out to
take them down, but she cannot be got
ready owins to the strike. The Thomas,
due here the 20th inst., will be sent out
with the educators. In this connection the
following letter is self-explanatory r .
"May 29/1901

sular Division. War Department;
. Sir â In reference to Commissioner Taft's tele
gram regarding the early transportation of
teachers from the United States to Manila, I
have the honor to Inform you that, in addition
to sending eleven teachers on the Lawton and
about seventy-five on the* Sheridan, instruc
tions have been given to the- general superin
tendent of the Army Transport 'Service, San
Francisco, Cal., to have the Sherman fitted up
as a teacher transport, with accommodations
for 125 female teachers ! and ' 400 or 500 male
teachers, the accommodations' to , be made as
comfortable as possible, the female' teachers to
occupy the cabin staterooms and! other com
fortable parts of the shipAthe men to be ac
commodated on the troop^decks with special
accommodations, bo they may be made as
comfortable as possible under the circum
stances, being furnished with meals equal to
those furnished In the saloon cabin, all of the
decks to be in common. Â¦. . â¢ .

The Toyo Klsen Kaisha's Hongkong
Maru arrived yesterday a day ahead of
t!rae. She was held a long time ' in the
stream by the quarantine officer owing to
a couple of "suspects" among the crew.
A number of the Japanese sailors and fire
men were sent to the quarantine station
and then the mail boat was ' allowed to
dock.
The Hongkong Maru came Into port fly
ing the British flag at her fore, and the
customs officers were wondering what was
the .matter, until they learned that Sir
Claude Macdonald, his Britannic Majesty's
Minister to Japan, was a passenger. Sir
Claude is. on his way to England. The
mail boat made a quick run from Yoko
hama to Honolulu, but was nearly six
days coming from the islands to San
Francisco. She brings 98 cabin and 3 sec
ond cabin passengers, besides 221 Chinese
and Japanese in the steerage.- The' cabin
passengers were:

a lad 17 years old, and demanded to know
where the money was hidden. The boy
laughed at them, so the ruffians strung
him up to the limb of a tree and riddled
his body with bullets. The father was
compelled to witness the death of his son
and then saw the robbers seize his wife.
When she refused to tell where the money
v/as hidden she was knocked down and
one of the gang was about to cut her
throat when the cry that the soldiers
were coming drove them off. It was
some of Vellaino's neighbors who, hear
ing the sound of firing, had come to his
assistance. Mrs. Vellaino soon recovered
from her injuries, but her husband seems
unable to forget the murder of their son.
He sold his. little farm and with; what
money the rebbers had left him he came
to San Francisco, and from here will re
turn to Switzerland.

| The work of repairing the wharves be
tween Main and Beale streets has been
let .to the City Street Improvement Com
pany. The contract price is $5946 50.
Thomas F. Casey has been reappointed
State wharfinger at Harrison-street
wharf. Mr. Casey has proved himself to
be an efficient and' courteous official, and
the merchants and shipowners will be glad
to hear of his reappointment.
The sailing of the steamship Colombia
for Central and South American ports has
been postponed until the 18th inst.
The Occidental and Oriental Steamship
Company's Gaelic sails at 1 p. m. to-day
for the Orient.

To-Day's Entries.
Doublet was the much shouted good
thing in the six furlong race, the fourth
on the card. Russell took this favorite to
the front early in the game, but nearly
lost to Prestidigitator by careless riding
from the paddock to the wire. "Presti"
made a strong finish, getting the place.
The fourth race brought more judicial
labors to the occupants of the stand, but
this time before the start. Rio Shannon,
owned by Thompson & Hagerman, was
entered. The attention of the judges was
called to the fact that Rio Shannon was
sick beyond the point or running, so an
investigation was made and a prompt rul
ing held on the facts. Rio was scratched,
the Thompson stable put under the ban
until January 1 and twenty minutes given
lor a new book. Hagerdon, the favorite at
even money, won this race handily, Rey
Dare getting the place and Byron Rose
the show.
Oscar Tolle, opening at 12 to 1 and clos
ing at 8 to 1, won the last race without
much trouble. Merops was an 8 to 5 fa
vorite and considered to be easy pick
ing. Merops was almost left â not by fault
of the starter but on account of eccentric
behavior. Merops broke when the barrier
was sprung, ran several yards and then
propped. At this time the bunch was far
in advance and Merops could not make
up the distance. Castake made the place
and Alicia the show.
Achilles had the speed yesterday and
when the three-quarter post was reached
led by a length, with Shell Mount follow
ing. Shell -Mount was riding easily an<l
as the two tore down the stretch it ap
peared for all the world as if the latter
would win with ease. Collis was not on
the winner. He displayed a strong pair
of hands whether controlled by ignorance
or malice aforethought.
Â£ir Hampton and Sweet Caporal were
the choices in the first race of the day,
the odds on the formjer spreading slowly
toward the closing. Sir Hampton with J.
McCarthy up worked up place by place
until the last burst, then winning out by
two lengths.
In the second race Burdock showed a
reversal of form, which the judges are in
vestigating. With Hoar in the saddle
Burdock won from Einstein, Hohenhole
and Guilder.
That Collis rode a wretched race there
can be ho doubt The judges charge his
performance to ignorance and inability
rather than to dishonesty. To the asphalt,
congregation and the occupants of the
stand with Shell Mount tickets in their
clothes it looked as if Collis worked the
brakes too diligently from the time the
stretch was reached until the wire was
passed. Collis certainly threw a winning
to the winds.
The judges' stand was the target for a
diJ-T-aUsfied public at the Oakland track
yesterday afternoon after the third race,
won by Achilles from Shell Mount. The
latter horse with Collis running the
brakes was the perfervid favorite, draw
ing a strong, Increasing play from the first
chalking till the horses went to the post.
Shell Mount was piloted in such a queer,
corkscrew manner that Achilles had no
trouble in winning. That is why the
crowd hooted and jeered . and gathered
about the stand with profane words on
their lips and .malice in their hearts." â¢". ;

The two teams then played a listless ex
hibition of four innings and the people
who remained to see them play left dis
gusted.
The only other feature of a very poor
exhibition was the , return of "Truck '
Eagan to the game. He played first for
Oakland and covered the bag in clever
style. Folowing is the score of the game
as far as it went:
â¢: SACRAMENTO. â¢
Strlcklett was the prime mover In the
insurrection. He went into the box in
flated with his own importance and was
sent skyward In the very first inning by
the Dudes, who hammered him for two
singles and a double, accepted a walk and
tallied five runs. The Senators were un
able to do anything in the second and
when Oakland came to bat they were
ready to Inflict further damage. Strick
le tt broke the rule that prevents a
pitcher taking two steps when delivering
the ball and^ Umpire Tyler gave Dunleavy
his base on balls. Strlcklett threw 'down
his glove in a passion and walked off the
field. There was a delay of five minutes
while the sulky youth was being induced
to resume pitching. He committed the*
same mistake a second time, either de
liberately or unconsciously, and another
man was allowed to walk. This was too
much for the Sacramento twirler and he
again left the game. The hoots and Jeers
that greeted his actions must have awak
ened a feeling of resentment in his heart.
but he failed to show it. Umpire Tyler
ordered the scorer to take time and he
warned Sacramento to. play ball. Hia
warning was supplemented by the crowd,
which yelled derisively at the visiting
legislators.
Devereaux then went into the box and
Oakland was retired in short order. Shee
han was first up for Sacramento and ha
tried talking back to the umpire and waa
promptly fined $5. The side was retired
in one. ' two, thre order. In the last of
the third the end came. Devereaux waa
in the box. He pitched a few times and
Tyler called it a ball. Devereaux thought
It should have been a strike. Ho turned
fiercely upon the umpire and was fined $5.
He tried back talk and was ordered off
the field. Tyler sent for a policeman,
but none could be found. There was
more delay and Oakland was given the
game by a score of 9 to 0. The spectators
hooted and hissed and then went down
and got rain checks which entitle them to
see another game.
Umpire Tyler acted the part of a school
master at the ball game yesterday, though
instead of inflicting the rod on half a doz
en sulky Senators, he fined a few and
then gave the. game to the Dudes by a
score of 9 to 0. The Sacramento aggrega
tion acted like so many overgrown coun
try bumpkins. They went to Recreation
Park with an ldea'of overawing or bluff
ing the umpire, but as the latter held the
high hand he stood pat and won every
time. â¢ .Â¦

Governorship of Territory of
Hawaii. y
HONOLULU, June 7.â United States
District Judge 'Morris M. Estee denies
the report in 3an Francisco that he in
tends to try for the Governorship of this
Territory upon the close of Governor
Dole's present term or in case of the con
tinued disability of the executive. Judge
Kstee states that he is not a citizen of the
Territory and that he does not feel that
he*ls qualified for the position of Gov
ernor. He is perfectly content to remain
here on the bench. "I have never -been
more in earnest in my life when I say to
you candidly," he is quoted as stating,
"that 1 do not v/ant to be appointed Gov
ernor of the Territory of Hawaii, nor do
I believe that President McKinley has
any intention of appointing me."
No Drawback on Shipments.
WASHINGTON. June 13.â The Secre
tary of the Treasury, acting under the re
cent decisions of the United States Su
preme Court in the Insular cases, has
held that no drawback can be allowed on
merchandise shipped to the ..Hawaiian
Islands after January 7, 1S98, these isl
ands having ceased to be a foreign coun-.
try within the meaning of the tariff laws
under the joint resolution of Congress
annexing the islands to the United States,
approved on that date.

JUDGE ESTEE SATISFIED
TO BjEMAIN ON THE BENCK
KANSAS CITY, June 13. âThe case of
Lulu Prince Kennedy, who killed her hus
band in January last because he refused
to live with her after a forced marriage,
will " probably go to the jury to-morrow
evening. The taking of testimony was fin
ished this afternoon and arguments will
begin to-morrow morning.
The defense sufferecNa second setback
to-day, when Judge Wofford ruled out a
conversation with Kennedy before the
killing tending to -corroborate evidence
offered- yesterday to prove that Kennedy
had admitted having ruined Lulu Prince.
Further expert testimony to prove that
the defendant was temporarily insane at
the time of the shooting was offered, and
Bert Prince, one of the quartet of alleged
conspirators, again took the stand, this
time for the defense ,and to prove an
alibi for himself. He denied that he had
predicted Kennedy's murder.
Mrs. Kennedy appeared much distressed
and cried frequently, during the day. ;

NEW YORK, June 13.-Axel Hellen
berg, a stock broker, has through his
counsel applied to Justice Gildersleeve, in
the Supreme Court, for a receiver for the
Cobre Grande Copper Company of Ari
zona and an injunction restraining it from
disposing of any of its property pending
action. The Justice allowed the counsel
interested until .to-morrow to submit
briefs. â¢ â¢ _
The controversy over the mines or the
company has extended over a period of
two years, principally in Arizona, and the
present suit is an attempt upon the part
of Mr. Hallenberg to get a hearing'ln the
New York courts.
Mr. Greene, one of the defendants in
the present suit, was in New York on
May 28, and personal service of the sum
mons and complaint in an application for
an injunction was made upon him at that
time, not only in his individual capacity,
but as president of the Greene Consoli
dated Copper Company. Since that time
strenuous efforts have Â«been 'made by his
counsel to have the case dismissed on the
ground of the want of jurisdiction and
that the controversy should be remitted
to Arizona. A motion to that "effect was
recently denied. ...

NEW YORK, : June 13.â The Brooklyn
Derby and the Tremont stakes, of
$10,000 each, v/ere the features' at the
Gravesend race track to-day and served
to draw a big: crowd. The conditions were
favorable for a good day's sport and a
good entry as well. Commando, AH
Green and Bellario were withdrawn from
the derby, leaving The Parader, Blues and
Bonnibert to fight for the rich prize. The
Parader, made the running for a* mile and
a quarter, with Blues well' up and Bonni
bert trailing. As they rounded into the
stretch The Parader ran out and Bonni
bert, closing with a rush, came on and
won easily by six lengths, while Blues
beat the tired favorite a .length for the
place. The time, 2:33 4-5, was fast and a
new track record* -.-...â¢ - .

San Francisco.A visiting prize-fighter gives us his assurance that
a vision of a fire engine is invariably an omen of
success for him. He might carry his faith in signs a
little further and gaze into the business end of a hose
while in action.
Ho Yow, the worthy Chinese Consul General at this
port, is making most vigorous, protest against the
landing of one of his countrymen in San Francisco.
Ho Yow might make as many of these protests as he
pleases and be assured that the more he made the
better we would like it.
The service, then, is desirable, feasible and useful
alike to the city, to the commercial interest and to
the Government. Such being the case the attention of
the officials at Washington should be at once called to
the matter, and the request for the establishment of
the service should be backed by every influence pos
eessed by our Congressmen, by our commercial bodies
and by the Federal officers at the port.
The usefulness of a prompt service between this city
and the Farallones is well understood by all who have
any interest in shipping news; npr would it be out
side the legitimate province of the Government to es
tablish it. In fact, it would be but an extension of
the lighthouse service. As was said by President Mc-
Xear of the Merchants* Exchange: "I believe it to
be within the province of this Government to con
struct such telegraphic system for the better protec
tion of our merchant marine. Also, it would be of
advantage to vessels in the Government service. They
could communicate with or receive orders without
the necessity of coming into the harbor. Vessels ar
riving off the port in distress, or requiring the ser
vices of a tug or other assistance, could, by signaling
the operator on the islands, communicate with the
city. Vessels coming to this coast in ballast with in
structions to get their orders off the harbor (to come
in or proceed to Portland or the Sound for their out
ward cargo) would be saved the inconvenience of de
lays. Such communication now is generally by the
pilot boats and necessitates considerable loss of time."
Of the feasibility of the service there can be no
question. Reports from Europe are to the effect that
the British have wireless telegraph stations at every
dangerous headland along their foggy and stormy
coasts. The Germans and the French also have
adopted the system to a considerable extent, and
only recently the news of the day illustrated the use
fulness of the service in the case of a steamer pass
ing a lightship off Dunk ; rk and receiving across the
water a wireless message to the effect that the illumi
nating machinery of the lightship was out of order
and that unless relief were sent there could be no light
on the ship that night. The steamer reported. the
news as soon as she reached port, and men were at
once sent to make repairs, so that the lightship
showed her signals as usual. In that case the -wireless
message may have saved from wreck some vessel
passing in the night and missing the familiar light
from the ship.
SENATORS, Representatives, merchants, ship
owners and citizens of influence, representing
commercial and industrial organizations, have
warmly commended the suggestion of The Call that
steps be taken to procure the establishment by the
National Government of a wireless telegraph service
between the Farallon Islands and this city. There
being unanimity of sentiment, it is reasonable to ex
pect something in the way of prompt and united ac
tion toward the desired end.

PERSONAL MENTION.
The establishment of so many new banks in a single
section of the country within seven months is an un
deniable proof of a rapid advance in the general pros
perity of the people. It is probable that none of the
new banks has a large capital, but the aggregate must
be considerable. The chief advantage is that the capi
tal is diffused throughout a wide extent of country
and that the benefits are widespread. Of course the
advantages to be derived from the banks are not to be
measured by the amount of their capital. Each of them
will serve as a financial reservoir for the collection
and distribution of the money in the community, en
abling the same funds to be used over and over again
and materially promoting every kind of industrial or
commercial entergrisc. *' , Â¦
When in connection with the extension of banking
facilities there is considered the improvement-going
on in the South in the way. of manufacturing and of
diversified rural industries there will be found ample
reason for believing that the blight of Bourbon poli
tics which has so long rested upon that section of the
Union will be removed by the force of prosperity it
self. The intelligence of the South cannot, fail to
perceive that the^ success of Bryanism; free silver and
free trade would ruin the bright. prospects that have
been raised by conservative government, sound money
and protection. It is therefore fair to expect that
hereafter it will be found supporting such policies in
the nation instead of making the South solid for any
and every kind of political folly that discontented
leaders can devise. ..
In a recent review of the situation the Atlanta Con
stitution says: "During the past seven months more
than one hundred banking institutions have been es
tablished in various parts of the South, and the dis
tribution among the various States is as follows:
Texas, 40; .Georgia, 13; Virginia, 13;' North Caro
lina, 11; Florida, 6; South Carolina, 9; Kentucky,
9; Arkansas, 6; 'Louisiana, 14; Tennessee, 5; Ala
bama, 5; Mississippi, 4, and Maryland, 5. While
some States have been more highly favored than
others, still there is no State throughout the section
which has not been favored to some'extent, and signs
of prosperity are general."
ONE of the more important factors in bringing
about the free silver craze in the Southern
States was the lack of adequate banking faciy
ties. By reason o.f that lack the people readily fell
into the belief that what they needed was more money,
and in that belief they would doubtless have supported
an unlimited issue of greenbacks just as readily, as
they supported the Western silver men had a green
back movement happened to be dominant at the time.
The need of a more general distribution of banking
and of money was. recognized by the framers of the
recent financial measures in Congress, and provision
was made for the establishment of small banks. The
South appears to have promptly profited by the op
portunity afforded by Republican legislation, and
there is no longer any complaint there of a lack of
currency or a lack of banks.
BANKING IN . THE SOUTH.
There have long been complaints of the increasing
extravagance in dress and living among the^ students
at Harvard and Yale. The ostentation of wealth which
mars so much of American life has intruded itself
into the very seats of learning, where it is supposed
\ he students are td be taught to respect higher things.
It now appears that fashionable colleges for girls in
New England are becoming as bad in that respect as
the universities. In place of the plain living and the
high thinking that formerly marked the colleges of
that section there has come a rivalry in dress and
ornaments carried to such an extent that some stu
dents wear jewels fitted for court ballrooms, and other
girls are tempted to steal them.
. The parents, the faculty and -the society that tol
erate a display of costly jewels' by college girls are
almost as much to blame for the foolish desire for
dress as was the silly girl who was caught by it and
fell. The moral duty of imposing restraint upon the
extravagance of the young must rest somewhere. It
will be a' badthing for New. England if the present
desire for a display of wealth corrupts her colleges
until they are no longer attractive to persons of
moderate means. "As matters stand at present parents
who cannot afford their daughters an elaborate array
of jewels and gowns to match for their college days
had better send them to Western colleges.
..Upon the showing thus made it is evident that the
,girl who stole the jewels is not the only fool in the
case. When young girlsat college wear diamond or
naments valued at $1500, and when gems and golden
jewelry are so common among the students that ar
ticles to the value of $5000 can be stolen by a single
thief, it is clear that college life at Smith is becoming
about as extravagant as that in the smart. set itself.
THE DESIRE, FOR DRESS.
RECENTLY a young woman of 21, the daughter"
of a family of eminence in New Jersey so
ciety and a student at Smith College, was '
arrested upon a charge of having stolen property |
from her fellow students. The girl is said to have
admitted the crime. .The value of the property stolen
is estimated at $5600^ Upward of fifty costly articles
of jewelry are missing, among them being a J diamond
ornament valued, at $1500. . ' ' .; / : :
With the exception' of her 'dishpnesty^the^young
woman is said to have an excellent character, and
her crime is attributed to desire, for dress. Orie re
port says: "It is known that she has pawned articles
in Springfield, Hartford, Philadelphia and New
Ydrk. It seems to be a clear case-of a foolish young
girl who desired to dress beyond her means and de^
liberately took the property of girls at the coliege,
pawned it and gratified her desires for luxurious'
living." â¢ â¢ : Â¦:%*!>

French Ideas in Politics.
The early century was a day of dueling.
Every gentleman In the South had met
his man. The story of Bladensburg-duel
ing field Is one of gloom. Dueling was
becoming almost as prevalent in the
North 'as in the South when the public
horror at the death of Alexander Hamil
ton by the hand of Aaron Burr caused a
great revulsion of feeling. Previous to
that tragedy New York had seen the
duels of Burr with John B. Church, of a
man named Eacker with Mr. Price. Then
Kacker killed Alexander Hamilton's old
est son, Philip, a youth of 19. Mr. Cole
man met Mr. Thompson; Mr. Riker met
one Swartwout, who had previously had
a meeting with De Witt Clinton, in which
five rounds of â¢ shots were exchanged,"
Clinton' wounding Swartwout each time
and the latter refusing to stop, until Clin
ton declared, he would not shoot again:
These duels were for the most trifling
causes. For Instance, Â¦ it was announced
that the "youth of the city" would meet
to address a letter of congratulation to
John Adams when he became President.
The next day an article appeared . in > a
newspaper -which' said the meeting was
presided over by "a stripling of 48 years,'
while Master Jemmy Jones, another boy
not quite 60, graced the assembly with his
presence." Jones .was deeply enraged at
this jocular sentence and sought out the
author, who Â¦ proved to be Judge Living
stone, the brother of Mrs.; Jay. Living-
Stone declared he was sorry to have given
offense, that he wrote it lightly. Jones
then, struck him- and tried .to pull his
nose. A challenge and duel followed, in
which Jones was instantly killed by a
shot in the heart. A settled gloom fell on
Livingstone, from: which, though he rose
to great* distinction, . he never recovered.''
In the Days of Dueling.
-This seemed so decisive that an annual
Inoculation was ordered in June, the time
of which should be posted in public
places, warned from house -to house, and
advertised in- the newspapers, and to
which strangers were invited. Due and
significant gratitude to God for "'so singu
lar and admirable a blessing" was shown
by permitting . the vaccination , to take
place in the meeting-house. The result
of all this was a law in Massachusetts
enforcing vaccination, and the loathsome
pest of smallpox never again .had a foot
hold in the State untilafter- the statute
was, unfortunately, repealed in 1836.

The most important reform of the early
years of the nineteenth century was the
establishment of temperance societies
.The first marked effect leading to this end
had been made by a tract ol Dr. Benja
min Rush of Philadelphia in 1S04 "An
Inquiry Into the Effect of Ardent Spirits
upon the Human Body and Mind." Ser
mons began to be preached on the sub
ject. In 180S a temperance society was
formed in Saratoga. The reform dragged
along slowly. Dr. Lyman Beecher In 1S01
sent an exhortation to a meeting of the
New York Synod. Other clergymen fol
lowed and other societies indorsed the
movement. .New societies were formedâ
"moral" societies, "farmer" societies
"abstinence" societies, temperance' soci
eties. Teetotal (a name adopted In 1833)
societies followed. Temperance tales were
printed. Reformed drunkards formed
Washingtonian societies, whose prophet
was John B. Gough. The cause had a lit
tle setback in the middle of the century
but, like all absolutely necessary reforms'
roso triumphant. , -

â¢ Among the many effects attributed to
the spread of French deism was the alter
ation in the observance of Sunday. This
[ did not progress without protest. Sunday
travel was still prohibited. In 1S02 Boston

In 1S10 the Postmaster General ordered
all postmasters who received mail on
Sunday to keep open offices, but his Puri
tan conscience directed his mailcarriers
to travel quietly and in no way to <Ji\-ert
attention from the church services. Re
monstrances arose. It was declared the
public convenience required travel and
mail transference, and that piou= corre
spondence, supplies to the needy, consola
tion to the afflicted and works of charity
were transmitted, as well as secular mat
ter, and should not be delayed. In 1&5
some zealots denounced exchanges of
ministers from town to town as violating
the Sabbath. Then Sabbath unions were
lormed and many reforms were effected.
Thus did public opinion waver.
In 1800 Dr. Waterhouse of Cambridge,
Mass., published a pamphlet on "The
Prospect of Exterminating the Small
pcx, etc. He at once vaccinated three
The newspapers of the early century
show how much excitement ;-there .was
over the introduction of vaccination as a
protection against smallpox. That disease
was a horrible .and universal scourge;
far more people had suffered from it
either through contagion or. inoculation
than had not had it. The latter class liv
ed in daily terror of disfigurement and
death. The words "pock-marked^* "pock
fretteh," "pock-broken," "pock-pitted,"
appear in nearly every personal descrip
tion" of the eighteenth century. It had
brought not only death but business dis
aster and ruin to the colonists. New
England had been devastated seven times
by the disease. Inoculation had con
trolled It but there seemed to be no hope
of exterminating it. The first public no
tice of vaccination was in 1799.

Federalists regarded with abhorrence all
Democrats and followers of Jefferson - as
French deists and drunkards. If they
did not deem every ' Democrat a profane
rascal, they were positive every profane
rascal was a Democrat. The story is told
of an election early in the . century at
Hadley. Mass., at which, when the ballots
were counted, one "Democratic vote was
found. TXie uproar and tumult Was great;
all wondered who the ungodly and abom
inable creature could have been who thus
disgraced .the entire town. After a time
a lean old man mounted' a. furry horse,
rode off to a safe distance and defiantly
called back, "I cast that 'ere Dimmercrat
vote," and galloped off amid a shower of
stones. Some years later Nathaniel Haw
thorne found himself in a somewhat sim
ilar position in Salem.

Mrs. Toynbee hopes' to publish a com
plete edition of. the letters, considerably
more than half of which have never been
printed. The Berry edition, published
nearly a century a?o, consisted of selec
tions representing only 343 letters.
Mrs. Paget Toynbee has had the good
fortune to discover in private hands the
originals of letters, about eight hundred
in number, addressed by Mme. de Deffand
to Horace Walpole between 1766 and 178U.
The majority are in the handwriting of
Mme. de Deffand's amanuensis, but sev
eral were written by Mme. de Deffand
herself in a wonderfully clear, large,
round hand, which she contrived to writa
after sha had become blind.

Miss Kathleen Haydn Green Is the first
Lady Mayoress who has published a book
during the tenure of her office.
Miss Green's first volume, a book of
verse, appeared in 1890. Her new book will
be published immediately by John Lane. It
is entitled "Twelve Allegories," among
the titles of which are "The Man- Who
Had Nothing or His Own," "Men Who
Have Sought the Unattainable" and "The
Woman \vho Wasted Her lears."
The famous detective, SherlocK Holmes,
will make his reappearance in the Sep
tember Strand Magazine. The book will
take the form not of short stories but of
a novel -of somewhere about fifty thou
sand words, about half of which are now
ready; No reference is to be made ut all
to the death of Sherlock Holmes, it being
merely, assumed that one of his earlier
experiences is being narrated. Dr. Conan
Doyle held out long against the most flat
tering offers, but was overcome at last.
A rather serjous accident which hap
pened to Jerome K. Jerome three weeKa
ago has been kept remarkably secret. He
was driving wltn Miss Lorlmer, herself a
novelist, behind a tandem in the neigh
borhood of his Wallingford house, when,
losing his eyeglasses, he ran into a bank.
Mr. Jerome smashed his ankle, and Miss
Lorimer's/ foot was seriously Injured. But
for all his helplessness Mr. Jerome is
quietly working over a novel on which he
has been intermittently engaged for three
years: He has made money, more espe
cially in America, with his play, "Miss
Hobbs," and now has a new piay finished,
which I hear contains some rather orig
inal and striking stage business. Daniel
Frohman was tne other day at his bed
side as a visitor to Mr. Jerome, of course
from motives of business as well as of
sympathy.
Mrs. Creighton has recovered from her
serious illness and is now engaged on the
biography of the late Bishop of London,
which the Messrs. Longmans hope to pub
lish in the autumn.

Life seems too short for men to attend to more
than the news and duties of each day, and the clan
gathering of last year seems as remote as the war
council of Highland chiefs the night before Culloden.
It was all of vital interest about one year ago, and
now it is forgotten. The judgment staked on the is
sue proved to be faulty, and now the resplendent
colonels aÂ«d others "have been." It. illustrates the
ephemeral nature of mere convention reputations.
Turning to the Republican National Convention, who
now remembers the name of the delegate that nomi
nated McKinley? At the moment of the action he
was the most envied of men, and by the estimate- put
upon the importance of his position one would have
thought that he was speaking himself into immortal
ity. Twelve months have passed and no one remem
bers who was so favored of the gods and envied of
men.
Rare days were they, when hyperbole, metaphor,
allegory and figures of speech sweat and swore like
overworked harvest hands, and there were "demon
strations" on the convention floor that lasted thirty
seven minutes by the watch.
scene. Again the bands play, men and women shout,
and then a colonel speaks. Listen! He says the party
skies have a rosy tinge and that the people in more
than thunder tones are demanding "the gold that pol
ished the winged sandals of Hermes and the silver
that glitters in the bow of Diana."
Passing over to the Democratic national conven
tion, the proceedings read like the roster of the staff
ot an army with banners. There were colonels born
to the title, others who won it in the Union and Con
federate ranks, and still others who laid aside their
war harness as privates and had promoted them
selves. The bright, resplendent militia colonel was
there, and the members of the junior order of real
colonels created by the Spanish war. It is a stirring
Mixed with it was the predicted downfall of the
republic and the coming of chaos and black night
unless Bryan were elected to turn the switch in time
to prevent the crash.
Then Major John Brown (colored), of Kansas also,
a veteran of the Cuban campaign, arose, and so did
the roof, when he pledged and plighted the colored
vote to the Silver Republicans. This black man was
followed by Mr. Crow of Dakota, and a long and
able lunged line of colonels, majors and captains,' all
pledging States and sections and solid blocks of
electoral votes to Bryan and Towne. K
Going into the Silver Republican convention one
sees Senator Teller, hoarse as a frog and hot as Yuma,
foretelling the triumph of silver and with the zeal
of an apostate denouncing his former party associates.
There are cries for Joseph Waters- of Kansas, and
he comes, full of fiery metaphors, which roused the
convention to a fine rage. For a brief space the
troubled Waters held the stage, and passed, where
no one knows. â¢ .
Those- were great days and brought into focus for
a time a large number of men who passed thence into
obscurity or have a precarious hold upon public 1 * at
tention.

proceedings of the Silver Republican and Democratic
conventions at Kansas. City pass like a procession
that is never to countermarch. , â¢

THE PASSING SHOW. 1
It is.not likely that any; of these schemes of bal
lot restriction will be accepted, but they are interest
ing as showing the diversity of Alabama sentiment on
the subject It will be seen that one of them would
exclude from the polls every citizen who cannot speak
English, which looks as if there were an anti-foreign
element in the State. '. The proposal that every man
who has a home shall have a vote shows that there is
some sense of appreciation of industrious and thrifty
negroes and a willingness. to let that class vote. ' The
proposal to give illiterates a colored ballot to count
for one-fourth vote is another evidence of the exist
ence of a glimmer of justice in the convention, for
while such a provision is riot Icommendable in it
self, it is certainly better than excluding educated
black men from the polls while admitting ignorant
white men.
The four bills referred to differ ( widely from one
another. One of them, submitted by Pillans' of Mo
bile, provides simply that no person shall be permitted
to vote or to register unless able to speak the Eng
lish language. Bulger of Tallapoosa would require
that all electors be able to read and understand the
constitution, but exempts from that requirement
every one who was a qualified voter January i, 1867.
Miller of Wilcox proposes a more elaborate system
of restriction. His plan makes qualifications as to
age, residence, education and taxable property to the
value of $300, exempts soldiers and descendants within
the fourth de'gree and persons who could vote in 1867
and their descendants within the second degree, fixes
1908 as the year in which all who offer to vote for the
first time' shall be possessed of the general qualifica
tions; also contains a poll tax clause and provides
that owners of homes shall be entitled to vote, irre
spective, of value. . '
An original and novel proposition was submitted by
Banks of Russell.. This plan, in addition to providing
a poll tax which must be paid as a preliminary to.vot
ing, disfranchising persons who sell their votes and
men who abandon their wives and children, contains
provision for a white and a colored ballot to be used
in all elections; ballot and booths are to be similar to
the Australian system, except that all electors who
can prepare their ballots without aid are to be given
ii (white ballot; those who need assistance in-prepar
ing the'ballot are tcbe given a colored ballot, but
are permitted to select their own marker; colored bal
lots count as one-fourth of a vote; lifter 1906 all per
sons coming into Alabama after' ratification of the
constitution and all who shall have become 21 years
of age in the meantime shall be 'able to read and write
before voting. â¢ -,

FINE CHURCH EDIFICE TO BE ERECTED IN HOLY REDEEMER PAR
ISH, FROM THE PLAN OF CHARLES J. DEVLIN, THE" ARCHITECT.
THE AUDITORIUM WILL SEAT 800 PEOPLE^
The exhibits at Mechanics' Pavilion are
to comprise displays from the different
branches of Epworth League work, from
religious organizations, from the counties
of the State, from private business houses
and corporations and from the >rallroads.
The missionary exhibit will contain rep
resentatives of the different peoples
among whom missionary work has been
done, together with specimens of their
native arts and products. The Prune
Growers' Association will have many
unique figures made from prunes, with a
multitude of illustrations of the uses to
which the fruit may be put. The South
ern Pacific Railroad will have an excep
tionallv One exhibit of redwoods. The
Santa ~Fe will show specimens of various
interesting curios to be found along Its
route. It is expected also that there will
be a display furnished by the Chinese Six
Companies, and this" feature is particular
ly desired on account of the interest taken
by visiting Easterners in the Chinese of
the Pacific .Coast. Many of the largest
local mercantile houses are also planning
to assist in a typical California exhibit.
Tickets will be placed on sale at the
special Epworth League rate in Chicago
on July 6 and will be sold for eight cotir
secutive days. The majority of the Ep
worth Leaguers are expected to come di
rect to San Francisco and from this city
make excursions to surrounding towns.
The Chamber of Commerce and the Meth
odists of Los Angeles will hold a recep
tion, however, to the Epworth Leaguers
who pass through there only July 13 and
14 on their way to San Francisco.
The hotel men are all looking forward
to the visit of the Epworth League. At
the present time the hotels are not crowd
ed, and the hotel men say that they will
have ample accommodations to handle the
vast army that will arrive. A register
has also been made of all the private
houses where rooms can be secured.
The handling of the baggage will not
be given exclusively to one transfer com
pany. The committee of arrangements
has decided that the moving of the trunks
is too big an undertaking for one concern.
All the reputable transfer companies will
be asked to pool on the business.
There will be an absence of all these
inconveniences this year, if the arrange
ments that were made in the railroad
bunding yesjerday are carried out to the
letter. The baggage of the Epworth
Leaguers will be brought over from the
Oakland mole in a special boat and not.
as is usually the custom, on a ferry boat.
Upon arrlying on this side the trunks will
be piled in small lots and located accord
ing to numbers.
When the Christian Endeavorers were
here three years ago the handling of the
baggage was a disgrace to a city with
the facilities of San â¢ Francisco. Ths
trunks on this occasion accumulated by
the hundreds in one big pile,- which as
sumed the proportions of a mountain.
Many of the owners of the trunks were
unable to find them and those that did
locate their Saratogas were unable to se
cure them, as numbers of the trunks were
at the bottom of the. pile and could not
be reached. Numbers of the Christian
Endeavorers never received their trunks
until they had returned to their homes In
the East. Â¦ ' Â¦ ,

His Health.
Sir Bosdin T. Leach, who arrived from
the Orient yesterday, is a well-known
business man of Manchester,, England.
He is president of the Manchester Steam
ship Company and Â¦ is touring the world
for his health.
In an interview at his rooms at the Pal
ace Hotel Jast evening Sir Bosdin stated
that times at present were most prosper
ous In England. He is most anxious to
impress on all American merchants the
importance of recognizing the value of
I Manchester as a center for the sale of
American products shipped to England.

Er. Buckley, Member of the Board of
Health, Characterizes Certain
Suggestions as Absurd.
Dr. V. P. Buckley, chairman of the
Health and Hospital Committee of the
Board of Health, sent a communication
to the Mayor yesterday anent the stric
tures on the management of the City and
County Hospital contained in the last
Grand Jury's report. Dr; Buckley han
dles, the inquisitorial body, without gloves
for making what he terms absurd sug
g-c-Etions. Among other things he says:
The puggeFtlon by the Grand Jury that con
valescent patients be made to cut up the trees
for lire wood is absurd. Persons strong enough
for that work are not kept in a hospital. The
puggestion that the election booths used by the
nurses be removed and the inclosure be . con
verted into a erassy lawn for the patients is
Impracticable. The pile of old iron complained
of had been there for many years and likely
will be for majiy more. No one will take It
away and we are considering the advisability
of digging a hole and burying It. Relative to
complaint about dogs, I will state that tho
assertion that they are annoyances to every
one and exist at the expense of the hospital
funds is not worthy of consideration.

FROPERTY-OWNERS ENTER
STRONG PROTEST AGAINST
PLANS for the new Catholic
church building of Holy Re
deemer parish have been adopted
and the erection of the sacred ed
ifice has already commenced. The
church Is to be located on the west side
of Diamond' street, near Eighteenth, and
will have a frontage of 65 feet and a depth
of 125 feet. There will be two floors to
the building, the lower being for hall pur
poses, Sunday school and entertainments.
The upper floor, where services will be.
held, will have a seating capacity of about
800. The church ceiling will be enriched
with panels and moldings. The walls will
be treated with pilasters and arches, pro T
ducing an arcade effect.'
Fourteen stained glass windows will af
ford abundance.of light in the auditorium
and add to the richness of the wall ef
fects. The sajictuary will be finished with
an elliptical coffer-paned ceiling and light
ed with three stained glass windows.
The entire edifice will be lighted
throughout by electricity. The interior
woodwork will be of yellow pine, var
nished and finished in the natural state.
The plastered walls and ceilings will be
tir-ted in such tones as to produce the
most harmonious effects. The building is
on classic lines of the Ionic style. The
height of the towero from sidewalk to the
top of the crosses is 90 feet. Charles J.
Devlin is the architect.
The parish is only six months estab
lished, and that so much progress has
been made is due to the earnestness and
i hard work of the Rev. Father Joseph P.
McQuaide, who is laboring earnestly to
raise the necessary funds to erect the
building.

| Quade is rector of the parish.

Secretary.
AGEXERAI* summary of th$ situa
tion in the strike of the journey
men butchers shows that neither
?Ide has rained or lost.
Butcher shops are still doing
business, but with a crippled force. Peo
ple are petting meat as they did before
the day the journeymen butchers went
out. From the interior men are coming
to handle the slaughtered product. Deliv
ery wagons have to wait upon them only
for their lack of deftness. There the sit
uation ends.
Butcher shops throughout the city were
running during the day. People got meat.
The Journeymen Butchers mot at the
Turk-street Temple during the afternoon
nnd received reports that certain un
named slaughterers and owners of large
bands of cattle would be able to supply
for three weeks a sufficient quantity of
beef and mutton to satisfy all demands.
The Wholesale Butchers' Association
held a meeting yesterday afternoon in
the office of Miller & Lux for the purpose
of outlining a plan of future action.
After the meeting Attorney Frohman,
representing the wholesalers, said:
"The wholesalers intend to treat this
us a live issue. Day after day we labored
t<-Â» avoid the present difficulties. Even
jifter the Retail Butchers' Association pe
titioned us to protect them against the
labor unions we delayed taking precipi
tate action in the hope that the trouble
would eventually end.
Agreed to the Schedule.
"The assertion on the part of the strikers
that the wholesalers were attempting to
l.reak the backbone of the journeymen's
union is absolutely false, for retailers
agreed to the schedule submitted by the
men and the shops v.-cre declared fair.
Some of the dealers, hoping to aid their
business, displayed union cards. This dis
play was optional, according to the agree
ment. The journeymen distributed thou
rands of cards, calling upon the public to
patronize only those shops displaying the
union signs. This was a boycott. In Oak
land the journeymen called upon the
vholrsalers to keep the union cards in
their places of business and that drew the
wholesalers into the fight.
"The retailers then made their appeal to
us. They peremptorily demanded that we
protect them from the unjust boycott
vhich was being levied against them by
ihe labor unions. Assistance was granted,
becaii?e if the butchers were forced to
submit to the domination of the unions, no
restaurant, no bakery nor individual could
hold out arainst their demands. They
could shut off the necessaries of life and
have us all at their mercy."
Abattoirs Are Idle.
Abattoirs at Butchertown, Berkeley,
Oakland and other places in the imme
diate vicinity of the bay are idle, but it is
not expected that this condition will last
long. The employing butchers, acting
ss a unit, have decided not to give meat
to any shop having union affiliations. As
the sunply of the various butcher shops
throughout this city and elsewhere re
mains in their hands the journeymen ap
parently have to override this situation.
It may be further stated that unless a re
cession is made regarding demands the
people of the city will experience the
meat famine predicted a few days ago.
A meeting of the executive committee
of the Journeymen Butchers' Association
was held last night to consider matters
effecting the strike. The meeting was
harmonious but failed to develop results.
A disposition was manifested to avoid
meeting the issue directly. A conference
of the Journeymen Butchers will be held
to-day at the Turk-street Temple.
IX the matter of the strike of the iron
workers no change was observable
yesterday. Headquarters has been
established at Third and Brannan
streets for the machinists' helpers.
It Is estimated that between all the
unions whose members are out on strike
about 6000 men are concerned. The wages
of the men. according to the various
branches of trade in which they are em
ployed, vary from $1 50 to $5 a day. A
conservative estimate of "the amount of j
wages now being lost by the strikers and |
others places the amount approximately
at $300.<XK>.
The Metal Trades Association has is
sued a Ptatement through George E. Dow
In which is laid down the conditions upon
which the individual grievance of each
man will be adjusted. The most striking
paragraphs of the announcement are con
tained in the following extracts:
Disapproving absolutely of strikes and lock
outs, the members of the association will not
arbitrate any question with men on strike.
Neither will this afsiriatinn countenance a
lockout on any arbitrable question unless ar
bitration has failed.
Employment â No discrimination will be made
against any member of any nociety or or
ganization. Every workman who elects to
work in a shon will be required to work peace
ably and harmoniously with all his fellow em
ployes.
Apprentices, helpers and handy menâ The
number of apprentices, helpers and handy men
to bft employed will be determined s5lely by
the employer. i

CHICAGO MACHINISTS WIN.
Six Firms Make a Settlement With
Their Striking Employes.
CHICAGO, June 13.â A break occurred
to-day in the ranks of the Chicago ma
chinery manufacturers whose employes
are on strike. Six firms employing 1.13
men reached a settlement with their strik
ing machinists and the men will return
to work to-morrow under am agreement
securing to them the nine-hour day and
an increase in wages acceptable to the
CIXCIXXATI. June 13.â The Machinists'
Union of Covington. Ky.. sent a letter
to Governor Beckham in which thfy ap
peal for an investigation of the action of
Professor J. P. Anderson of the Kentucky
State College, who, they claim, is sending
students from the mechanical department
of the college to Cincinnati to take the
places of the striking machinists. The of
ficers of the union assert that ten students
are now at work in various shops in Cin
cinnati, and that they are working with
out any pay whatever.
CAPTAIN LZAIiE GIVES
PLEASANT BAY EXCURSION
Jolly Crowd on Steamer Caroline
Extends Greeting to the Bat
tleship Oregon.
Captain Leale's stanch little craft, the
Caroline, carried a gay crowd around the
bay last night. The jolly skipper asked
his friends to come with him and cele
brate the return of the battleship Oregon,
and a celebration it was in every respect.
For fully an hour the Caroline steamed
about the "pride of the navy." From the
decks of the little steamer was wafted the
strains of sweet music, which roused to
life the jackies occupying: soft spots on
the deck of the "bulldog." Three hearty
cheers for the Oregon were given by the
passengers on the Caroline. They were
answered with three ringing ones from
the battleshir>.
Billy Hinds was on the Caroline and gav.i
his famous "Laughing Song" and was
heartily applauded by the bluejackets.
He was followed by Homer Henley, who
rendered "The Holy City" with much suc
cess.
As a parting salute the choruses of sev
eral popular ditties were then sung by
Captain I^eale's passengers.
The Caroline then steamed for its berth
at Jackson-street wharf. On the return
trip light refreshments were served.
Those who made the trip were loud in
their nraises of Captain Leale's hospital
ity.

NEW YORK, June 13.â The Commercial Ad
vertiser's Londcn financial cablegram says:
The reduction of the bank rate to-day from
ZVt to 3 per cent caused general surprise, for
although the increasing ease ,in money fore
shadowed the possibility of such action, it was
not generally expected that a reduction would
be actually made to-day. The stock market's
view of the matter Is that the bank directors
have anticipated . an early peace in the Boer
war. But the easy condition both of the bank
and stock market affords ample, explanation
of to-day's action. . â¢ Â¦

move much. Union Pacific, Baltimore and

York buying with the Continent slightly as
sisting the first two. . ,

Chicago Livestock Market.
NEW YORK, June 13.â Reports from corre
spondents of R. G. Dun & Co. in the principal
Eheep raising States indicate that the season
has been most satisfactory, both as to size
and condition of clip, but complaints of prices
are very general. On many ranches the shear
ing is about completed and a yield somewhat
larger than the one preceding Is assured. -At
far Western, points there is much wool still
on the sheeps' backs, but animals are healthy
and no difficulty Is anticipated. v Not only Is
the quantity larger this season but the quality
Is better and wool so much cleaner that tosses
in scouring will be less than usual. A few re
ports indicate that growers have already sold
much of their wool.' but in the majority of
cases there is no desire to accept current prices.
This is especially true of the wealthier ranch
owners who can afford to hold their product.
Buyers are not alarmed by the tendencyto re
fuse offers which are about a cant lower than
a month ago and express.the opinion that still
easier, terms may be secured later. Consider
ing current stocks at the East and conditions
in the manufacture, prospects for higher prices
are not encouraging, notwithstanding the
decline of the past year.. ,
Goo.i~ Outlook 1 or Wool.

Mrs. OIlie Dlxon yesterday reported to
the police that Bhe had been robbed of a
pair of $300 diamond earrings on Wednes
day night. In a house at 127 Eleventh
street. According to the woman's story,
she went to the house at 10 p. m. on
Wednesday and rented a room. Yesterday
morning when she awoke Mrs. Dlxon
found that her earrings were missing,
though the door of the room was locked.
Her Diamond Earrings Hissing.
Judge Seawell made an order yesterday
directing Annie A. Sousa to reconvey to
Mrs. Catherine Casey an improved lot on
Antonio street, near Jones. Mrs. Casey
conveyed the property in question to Mrs.
Sousa a few months ago and subsequent
ly filed suit to recover, the. same on the
ground that Mrs. Sousa exercised undue
influence to obtain the deed. Judge Sea
well, found that Mrs.. Casey's allegations
were based on fact and entered judgment
accordingly. . , . i .
Undue Influence Used.

The Supervisors' Hospital and Health
Committee yesterday rejected the bids of
the William S. Merrill Chemical Company
for drugs to be furnished the Health De
partment, on the ground that it is an
Eastern firm. The bids were submitted
by E. B. Hudson, as manager of the con
cern. According to the notice in the pro
posal. It provides that no bid except from
a merchant who is a regular dealer In the
articles enumerated will be received. The
committee ruled that it would be an in
justice to local concerns to accept bids of
Eastern firms. -^ â¢ ..-.
Rules Against Eastern Bids.

Frederick John Buhler, a shoemaker,
Â¦who hH<l a small shop at 312 Jackson
street, was found 1> ing dead on the floor
of the sleeping room back of the shop
yesterday afternoon. The eyes and a part
of the left hand and foot had been eaten
away by rats. It is believed that the body
must have beer, lying there for two days
at least. Buhler was a native of Switzer
land. 75 years old. and a miser. He lived
In an extremely filthy manner.; A will
dated three years ago was found among
his papers, in which he bequeaths to the
San Francisco Protestant Orphan Asy
lum all his property, without mentioning
of what it consists. No property was
found on the premiss. Â¦ . " -

Ezra Kendall,- the- clever monoloÂ«rist
who Is appearing at the Orpheum, had
the proud distinction yesterday afternoon
of officiating as best man and bridesmaid
at a quiet wedding that took place at the
Palace . Hotel. John W. Kitey, a promN
nent young business man of Boston and
Lou Umsted were the happy couple mar
ried and their courtship was short.
The bride and bridegroom first met Of
the train coming "West about two weeks
ago. It was love at first sight with them
and upon arriving In this city they decid
ed to get married, h Early yesterday morn
ing Kiley. accompanied by Kendall, ap
plied, for a marriage license at the City
Hall. After procuring the desired doc-i
ment they returned to thf Palace, and
securing the service of a minister had the
matrimonial knot tied. Kendall was the
only witness, and from all that can be
learned he was a great success. - â¢ ' Â¦â¢. ,,

nently in San Jose, where , the newly
made husband will engage. in business.
